<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Gong Hyo-jin</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

</div>

<p><b>Gong Hyo-jin</b> (born April 4, 1980) is a <a href="/wiki/South_Korean" title="South Korean">South Korean</a> actress. She is best known for her leading role in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Crush_and_Blush" title="Crush and Blush">Crush and Blush</a></i> (2008), as well as for her popular television series <i><a href="/wiki/Sang_Doo!_Let%27s_Go_to_School" title="Sang Doo! Let's Go to School">Sang-doo! Let's Go to School</a></i> (2003), <i><a href="/wiki/Thank_You_(TV_series)" title="Thank You (TV series)">Thank You</a></i> (2007), <i><a href="/wiki/Pasta_(TV_series)" title="Pasta (TV series)">Pasta</a></i> (2010), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Greatest_Love" title="The Greatest Love">The Greatest Love</a></i> (2011), <i><a href="/wiki/Master%27s_Sun" title="Master's Sun">Master's Sun</a></i> (2013), <i><a href="/wiki/It%27s_Okay,_That%27s_Love" title="It's Okay, That's Love">It's Okay, That's Love</a></i> (2014), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Producers_(TV_series)" title="The Producers (TV series)">The Producers</a></i> (2015), and <i><a href="/wiki/Jealousy_Incarnate" title="Jealousy Incarnate">Jealousy Incarnate</a></i> (2016).</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gong_Hyo-jin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gong Hyo-jin was born in 1980 in <a href="/wiki/Sinwol-dong" title="Sinwol-dong">Sinwol-dong</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gangseo_District,_Seoul" title="Gangseo District, Seoul">Gangseo District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, South Korea. When she was a junior in high school, she moved to <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> with her mother and younger brother, while her father remained in Korea to support the family. Gong attended high school at <a href="/wiki/John_Paul_College_(Brisbane)" title="John Paul College (Brisbane)">John Paul College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Gong has spoken fondly of her memories of her time there, and in 2011 she was designated as one of the goodwill ambassadors for "Year of Friendship," the 50th anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Bilateralism" title="Bilateralism">bilateral relations</a> between Australia and South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>After three years in Australia, the Gong family moved back to Korea in 1997 due to the <a href="/wiki/1997_Asian_financial_crisis#South_Korea" title="1997 Asian financial crisis">IMF crisis</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gong_Hyo-jin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_work">Early work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gong_Hyo-jin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon her return to Korea, Gong began working as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Model_(profession)" title="Model (profession)">model</a>. She appeared in advertisements, notably the "Happy to Death" commercial for telecom 700-5425. After a year and a half of modeling, she made her acting debut in a supporting role in <i><a href="/wiki/Memento_Mori_(film)" title="Memento Mori (film)">Memento Mori</a></i>. Co-directed by <a href="/wiki/Kim_Tae-yong" title="Kim Tae-yong">Kim Tae-yong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Min_Kyu-dong" title="Min Kyu-dong">Min Kyu-dong</a>, the horror film reinvented its genre with its fresh approach on teenage femme sexuality and its destructive force, melding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sapphism" title="Sapphism">sapphism</a> and the supernatural at a girls' high school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Although it was not a box office hit in 1999, the film is frequently cited by young Korean filmmakers and cinema fans as a modern-day classic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paquet_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-paquet-10">[10]</a></sup> At first Gong wasn't serious about acting and couldn't wait for filming to be over, but <i>Memento Mori'</i>s critical success encouraged her to continue acting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl2_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-gl2-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charm_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-charm-12">[12]</a></sup> and she followed that with a role in 2000 <a href="/wiki/Sitcom" title="Sitcom">sitcom</a> <i>My Funky Family</i>.</p>
<p>In 2001 she was cast in the 50-episode television series <i>Wonderful Days</i>, where she played a bus conductor with a one-sided crush on <a href="/wiki/Ryoo_Seung-bum" title="Ryoo Seung-bum">Ryoo Seung-bum</a>'s character (Gong and Ryoo later won Best New Actress and Best New Actor in the TV category at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baeksang_Arts_Awards" title="Baeksang Arts Awards">Baeksang Arts Awards</a>). After appearing in small roles in <a href="/wiki/Jang_Jin" title="Jang Jin">Jang Jin</a>'s comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Guns_%26_Talks" title="Guns &amp; Talks">Guns &amp; Talks</a></i> and teen martial arts flick <i><a href="/wiki/Volcano_High" title="Volcano High">Volcano High</a></i>, the young actress had her breakthrough year in 2002, landing lead roles in <i><a href="/wiki/Emergency_Act_19" title="Emergency Act 19">Emergency Act 19</a></i> (which featured <a href="/wiki/Cameo_appearance" title="Cameo appearance">cameos</a> by many <a href="/wiki/K-pop" title="K-pop">K-pop</a> artists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>), and <i>A Bizarre Love Triangle</i> (also known as <i>Taekwon Girl</i>). But it was her raw, complex performance in <i><a href="/wiki/Ruler_of_Your_Own_World" title="Ruler of Your Own World">Ruler of Your Own World</a></i> that grabbed the industry's attention. The TV series was praised for its realistic writing and strong acting, earning it "mania drama" (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cult_hit" title="Cult hit">cult hit</a>) status in Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> That same year, she again acted opposite Ryoo in <i><a href="/wiki/Conduct_Zero" title="Conduct Zero">Conduct Zero</a></i> (also known as <i>No Manners</i>), earning praise for her role as the tough-talking "boss" of the girls' high school. The 1980s-set retro comedy was well received by both audiences and critics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cz_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-cz-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2003 series <i>Snowman</i> paired Gong with <a href="/wiki/Cho_Jae-hyun" title="Cho Jae-hyun">Cho Jae-hyun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Rae-won" title="Kim Rae-won">Kim Rae-won</a>, in a controversial plot about a girl who falls in love with her older brother-in-law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> She then returned to more mainstream fare in <i><a href="/wiki/Sang_Doo!_Let%27s_Go_to_School" title="Sang Doo! Let's Go to School">Sang-doo! Let's Go to School</a></i>, helmed by TV director <a href="/wiki/Lee_Hyung-min" title="Lee Hyung-min">Lee Hyung-min</a>, whom she had previously worked with in a <a href="/wiki/Drama_City" title="Drama City">Drama City</a> episode. Gong played a high school teacher who meets her childhood sweetheart again, now a gigolo and single dad with a sick daughter. Known for being the acting debut of pop singer <a href="/wiki/Rain_(entertainer)" title="Rain (entertainer)">Rain</a>, the drama did well in the ratings, and Gong won several awards at the <a href="/wiki/KBS_Drama_Awards" title="KBS Drama Awards">KBS Drama Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kbs2003_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-kbs2003-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>But in 2004 to 2005, Gong entered a career slump. She was dissatisfied with the scripts she was getting, and felt she was being <a href="/wiki/Typecasting_(acting)" title="Typecasting (acting)">typecast</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ingenue_(stock_character)" title="Ingenue (stock character)">ingenue</a> roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl2_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-gl2-11">[11]</a></sup> Cast as another high school teacher in <i><a href="/wiki/Hello_My_Teacher" title="Hello My Teacher">Biscuit Teacher and Star Candy</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and a scientist in <i><a href="/wiki/Heaven%27s_Soldiers" title="Heaven's Soldiers">Heaven's Soldiers</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Gong longed to portray meatier, "real women" roles, but she was unwilling to do <a href="/wiki/Nudity_in_film" title="Nudity in film">nudity in film</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_success">Critical success</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gong_Hyo-jin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Critical success">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2006, <i>Memento Mori</i> director <a href="/wiki/Kim_Tae-yong" title="Kim Tae-yong">Kim Tae-yong</a> (whom Gong considers her mentor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-queen_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-queen-23">[23]</a></sup>) offered her a part in <i><a href="/wiki/Family_Ties_(film)" title="Family Ties (film)">Family Ties</a></i>, a role he had written specifically for her. With the original Korean title "Birth of a Family," the film drew widespread acclaim as a delicately observed, cross generational look at unconventional families. The cast was praised for their brilliant acting, with reviews singling out Gong's portrayal of an angry young woman in a deeply troubled relationship with her mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> She received a Best Actress nomination from the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Film_Awards" title="Korean Film Awards">Korean Film Awards</a>, and also shared Best Actress honors with co-stars <a href="/wiki/Moon_So-ri" title="Moon So-ri">Moon So-ri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Go_Doo-shim" title="Go Doo-shim">Go Doo-shim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Hye-ok" title="Kim Hye-ok">Kim Hye-ok</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki_International_Film_Festival" title="Thessaloniki International Film Festival">Thessaloniki International Film Festival</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Family Ties</i> marked a turning point in her career,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl2_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-gl2-11">[11]</a></sup> and gave Gong a renewed passion for acting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-queen_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-queen-23">[23]</a></sup> In 2007 she returned to television in <i><a href="/wiki/Thank_You_(TV_series)" title="Thank You (TV series)">Thank You</a></i>, written by <i><a href="/wiki/Sang_Doo!_Let%27s_Go_to_School" title="Sang Doo! Let's Go to School">Sang-doo</a></i> scribe <a href="/wiki/Lee_Kyung-hee" title="Lee Kyung-hee">Lee Kyung-hee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Several actresses had turned down the unglamorous role of a single mother with an <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV</a>-positive daughter and a grandfather with <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a>; another reason being that this was lead actor <a href="/wiki/Jang_Hyuk" title="Jang Hyuk">Jang Hyuk</a>'s comeback after his draft-dodging scandal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Despite little hype, <i>Thank You</i> became a modest hit and rose to number one in its timeslot. Viewer response to the drama had been mostly heartwarming and life-affirming, which Gong said she treasures. The maternal role served to soften and feminize her image, but Gong was also praised for her nuanced portrayal that grounded her character in reality, helping to prevent the drama from being overly maudlin or saccharine.</p>
<p>After <i>Thank You</i>, Gong went back to film and took on supporting roles for the opportunity to work with some of the most talented directors in <a href="/wiki/Chungmuro" title="Chungmuro">Chungmuro</a>. She played a coolly unsentimental ex-girlfriend in <a href="/wiki/Hur_Jin-ho" title="Hur Jin-ho">Hur Jin-ho</a>'s melodrama <i><a href="/wiki/Happiness_(2007_film)" title="Happiness (2007 film)">Happiness</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> a concerned fiancee in <a href="/wiki/Lee_Myung-se" title="Lee Myung-se">Lee Myung-se</a>'s stylistic psychodrama <i><a href="/wiki/M_(2007_film)" title="M (2007 film)">M</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and a spy in <a href="/wiki/Ryoo_Seung-wan" title="Ryoo Seung-wan">Ryoo Seung-wan</a>'s action comedy/parody <i><a href="/wiki/Dachimawa_Lee" title="Dachimawa Lee">Dachimawa Lee</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Strong performances in varied roles helped cement Gong's reputation as a serious actress, but <i><a href="/wiki/Crush_and_Blush" title="Crush and Blush">Crush and Blush</a></i> in 2008 would become her most high-profile movie yet. Hailed by critics as one of the most original Korean films in recent years, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Kyoung-mi" title="Lee Kyoung-mi">Lee Kyoung-mi</a>'s feature directorial debut was divisive, and though its box office performance was a disappointment, it acquired a sort of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cult_status" title="Cult status">cult status</a> among Korean <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cinephile" title="Cinephile">cinephiles</a>. The black comedy was a showcase for Gong, who transformed herself into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Misanthropic" title="Misanthropic">misanthropic</a> <a href="/wiki/Antihero" title="Antihero">antiheroine</a> with an unattractively blushing red face, frizzy hair, dowdy clothes, and a chronic case of inferiority complex and hopeless delusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Gong had wavered at first when presented with the script, given the character's excesses. Reportedly urged on by fellow actress <a href="/wiki/Jeon_Do-yeon" title="Jeon Do-yeon">Jeon Do-yeon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> she eventually accepted and threw herself into the role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl2_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-gl2-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Park_Chan-wook" title="Park Chan-wook">Park Chan-wook</a>, who produced the film, praised Gong's subtle emotional variations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and told her that she might never be able to top this performance, joking that she should retire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> She won numerous acting awards in Korea, among them Best Actress trophies from the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Film_Awards" title="Korean Film Awards">Korean Film Awards</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Director%27s_Cut_Awards" title="Director's Cut Awards">Director's Cut Awards</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and Women in Film Korea Awards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> She also received nominations from the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Dragon_Film_Awards" title="Blue Dragon Film Awards">Blue Dragon Film Awards</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baeksang_Arts_Awards" title="Baeksang Arts Awards">Baeksang Arts Awards</a>, as well as a Rising Star Award from the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Asian_Film_Festival" title="New York Asian Film Festival">New York Asian Film Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mainstream_popularity">Mainstream popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gong_Hyo-jin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mainstream popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gong_Hyo-jin_from_acrofan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="270" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Gong_Hyo-jin_from_acrofan.jpg/150px-Gong_Hyo-jin_from_acrofan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Gong_Hyo-jin_from_acrofan.jpg/225px-Gong_Hyo-jin_from_acrofan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Gong_Hyo-jin_from_acrofan.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gong_Hyo-jin_from_acrofan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gong Hyo-jin in April 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After starring with close friend <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shin_Min-ah" title="Shin Min-ah">Shin Min-ah</a> in the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Independent_film" title="Independent film">indie</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Sisters_on_the_Road" title="Sisters on the Road">Sisters on the Road</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Gong played an aspiring chef in romantic comedy series <i><a href="/wiki/Pasta_(TV_series)" title="Pasta (TV series)">Pasta</a></i> in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Originally written as the usual brash and spunky rom-com heroine, Gong thought it would be boring and cliched to play her as such, and instead made the significant acting decision to play against type by creating the character as an ordinary girl who was seemingly meek, but had a quiet strength and slyly got her way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl2_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-gl2-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pasta2_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-pasta2-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Her chemistry with co-star <a href="/wiki/Lee_Sun-kyun" title="Lee Sun-kyun">Lee Sun-kyun</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and the drama's breezy atmosphere propelled it to the top of the ratings chart<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and received positive feedback from viewers.</p>
<p>Defying easy categorization into the actress dichotomies of innocent (<a href="/wiki/Choi_Ji-woo" title="Choi Ji-woo">Choi Ji-woo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Song_Hye-kyo" title="Song Hye-kyo">Song Hye-kyo</a>) or sexy (<a href="/wiki/Kim_Hye-soo" title="Kim Hye-soo">Kim Hye-soo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uhm_Jung-hwa" title="Uhm Jung-hwa">Uhm Jung-hwa</a>), Gong belonged to a third, very minor group of eccentrics that also include <a href="/wiki/Kang_Hye-jung" title="Kang Hye-jung">Kang Hye-jung</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bae_Doona" title="Bae Doona">Bae Doona</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Though not a typical beauty, after the success of <i><a href="/wiki/Pasta_(TV_series)" title="Pasta (TV series)">Pasta</a></i>, Gong was given the label <i>Gongvely</i> by the press, a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of her surname and the English word "lovely."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-queen_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-queen-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In an emerging pattern of alternating mainstream TV series with riskier big-screen projects, Gong starred in <i>Rolling Home with a Bull</i>, another low-budget <a href="/wiki/Independent_film" title="Independent film">indie</a> adapted from Kim Do-yeon's novel. She played a widow traveling with her poet ex-boyfriend in <a href="/wiki/Yim_Soon-rye" title="Yim Soon-rye">Yim Soon-rye</a>'s part-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> meditation, part-<a href="/wiki/Road_movie" title="Road movie">road movie</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, Gong acted opposite <a href="/wiki/Cha_Seung-won" title="Cha Seung-won">Cha Seung-won</a> in the TV series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Greatest_Love" title="The Greatest Love">The Greatest Love</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl2_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-gl2-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Written by the <a href="/wiki/Hong_Sisters" title="Hong Sisters">Hong Sisters</a>, the romantic comedy is set in the entertainment industry and about an unlikely romance between a has-been pop-star and a top actor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The series was a big hit with audiences, resulting in increased popularity for Cha and Gong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-queen_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-queen-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> She was also praised for her naturalistic, no-nonsense acting, which served to balance Cha's wacky antics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <i>The Greatest Love</i> swept the <a href="/wiki/MBC_Drama_Awards" title="MBC Drama Awards">MBC Drama Awards</a>, including a Top Excellence Award for Gong (her third consecutive, after <i>Thank You</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mbc2007_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-mbc2007-74">[74]</a></sup> and <i>Pasta</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mbc2010_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-mbc2010-75">[75]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mbc2011_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-mbc2011-76">[76]</a></sup> Gong later won Best Actress for TV at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baeksang_Arts_Awards" title="Baeksang Arts Awards">Baeksang Arts Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>She worked again with <a href="/wiki/Kim_Tae-yong" title="Kim Tae-yong">Kim Tae-yong</a> for <i>Beautiful 2012</i>, a series of four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micro_Movies" title="Micro Movies">Micro Movies</a> produced by Chinese internet platform <a href="/wiki/Youku" title="Youku">Youku</a>. that explore the concept of "what is beautiful?". In Kim's <a href="/wiki/Short_film" title="Short film">short film</a> <i>You Are More Than Beautiful</i>, <a href="/wiki/Park_Hee-soon" title="Park Hee-soon">Park Hee-soon</a> plays a man who hires an actress named Young-hee (Gong) to pretend to be his fiancée when he introduces her to his dying father in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeju_Island" title="Jeju Island">Jeju Island</a>. The short's Korean title translates to "Her Performance," and in a charming scene, Young-hee slips into the hospital room and sings a <a href="/wiki/Pansori" title="Pansori">Korean opera song</a> to the unconscious father (in a room with five other seriously ill elderly men).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <i>You Are More Than Beautiful</i> later received a theatrical release in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Uninterested in stereotypical pretty roles, Gong said she preferred playing multi-faceted women, like the laidback, unpredictable female lead with unshaved armpit hair in <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Fiction" title="Love Fiction">Love Fiction</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charm_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-charm-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Known for her candor on set and in public, Gong openly admitted that she had problems with her character and took her complaints to its director Jeon Kye-soo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Though Gong said she would rather continue making small-scale films rather than do a shallow blockbuster,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <i>Love Fiction</i> was her most commercial feature yet, and broke even at more than 1.7 million admissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>She then reunited with <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Fiction" title="Love Fiction">Love Fiction</a></i> co-star <a href="/wiki/Ha_Jung-woo" title="Ha Jung-woo">Ha Jung-woo</a> in <i>577 Project</i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Documentary" title="Documentary">documentary</a> that follows a group of actors walking 577 kilometers (358 miles) across the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013 Gong starred in comedy film, <i><a href="/wiki/Boomerang_Family" title="Boomerang Family">Boomerang Family</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Park_Hae-il" title="Park Hae-il">Park Hae-il</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoon_Je-moon" title="Yoon Je-moon">Yoon Je-moon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Youn_Yuh-jung" title="Youn Yuh-jung">Youn Yuh-jung</a>, adapted from Cheon Myung-kwan's novel <i>Aging Family</i> about a grown-up trio of siblings who embark on a series of misadventures after they move back in their mother's home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Gong said she felt catharsis from her character's constant cursing, and pleasure from acting in an ensemble whose actors share great chemistry with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Veteran actress Youn said that Gong's role, a twice-divorced single mother, was perfect for her that Youn couldn't imagine anyone else playing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hong_Sisters" title="Hong Sisters">Hong Sisters</a> cast her again in their next series <i><a href="/wiki/Master%27s_Sun" title="Master's Sun">Master's Sun</a></i>, a romantic comedy with horror elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Costar <a href="/wiki/So_Ji-sub" title="So Ji-sub">So Ji-sub</a> praised Gong as "the best Korean actress currently working in romantic comedy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The drama series was a commercial hit and renewed So and Gong's domestic and international popularity.</p>
<p>In 2014, Gong starred as a psychiatrist who falls for a mystery novelist with <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a> (played by <a href="/wiki/Jo_In-sung" title="Jo In-sung">Jo In-sung</a>) in the romantic drama series <i><a href="/wiki/It%27s_Okay,_That%27s_Love" title="It's Okay, That's Love">It's Okay, That's Love</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> She said she chose the project because of screenwriter <a href="/wiki/Noh_Hee-kyung" title="Noh Hee-kyung">Noh Hee-kyung</a>, who had also written a drama Gong appeared in a decade ago, <i>Wonderful Days</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Despite lackluster ratings, <i>It's Okay, That's Love</i> ranked third on the year-end Content Power Index and received praise for addressing the discrimination and social stigma attached to people with mental health issues and other minorities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stage_debut">Stage debut</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gong_Hyo-jin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Stage debut">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gong then made her stage debut in the <a href="/wiki/Willy_Russell" title="Willy Russell">Willy Russell</a> play <i><a href="/wiki/Educating_Rita" title="Educating Rita">Educating Rita</a></i>, which depicts the relationship during the course of a year between a young working class hairdresser and a middle-aged university lecturer (played by <a href="/wiki/Jeon_Moo-song" title="Jeon Moo-song">Jeon Moo-song</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, she starred in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Producers_(TV_series)" title="The Producers (TV series)">The Producers</a></i>, a variety drama series written by <a href="/wiki/Park_Ji-eun" title="Park Ji-eun">Park Ji-eun</a> who also wrote the hugely successful <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/My_Love_From_the_Star" title="My Love From the Star">My Love From the Star</a></i>. The drama revolved around the lives of the employees working in the network's variety department, and Gong plays a <i><a href="/wiki/Music_Bank_(TV_series)" title="Music Bank (TV series)">Music Bank</a></i> variety show producer who has been working in broadcasting for 10 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The drama drew solid viewership ratings nationwide throughout its run with a peak viewer rating of 17.7 percent and topped the Content Power Index (CPI) rankings for the most influential dramas of the first half of 2015. The drama also gained popularity internationally having been sold to Asian countries such as China, Japan, Thailand, Indonesia, Vietnam, Philippines, Taiwan, and Hong Kong, as well as countries in Commonwealth of Independent States.</p>
<p>Gong was then cast in <i>Missing Child</i>, in the role of a babysitter who one day disappears with someone else's child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, Gong was cast in thriller film <i>Single Rider</i> with <a href="/wiki/Lee_Byung-hun" title="Lee Byung-hun">Lee Byung-hun</a>. She played a former violinist who lives in Australia with her son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> She then made her drama comeback with SBS rom-com <i><a href="/wiki/Jealousy_Incarnate" title="Jealousy Incarnate">Jealousy Incarnate</a></i> opposite <a href="/wiki/Jo_Jung-suk" title="Jo Jung-suk">Jo Jung-suk</a>, playing a weather broadcaster.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_activities">Other activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gong_Hyo-jin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Other activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2010 Gong published a collection of essays on the environment titled <i>Gong Hyo-jin's Notebook</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a>: <span lang="ko-Hang">공책</span>; <a href="/wiki/Revised_Romanization_of_Korean" title="Revised Romanization of Korean">RR</a>: <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Korean transliteration">Gong Chek</span></i>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Word_play" title="Word play">play</a> on her surname and the Korean word for "notebook"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>). In it Gong gives a look inside her personal lifestyle habits, and offers practical and simple tips on going green such as using shower gels and shampoo sparingly, driving a small car, shopping at flea markets, reusing plastic bags, unplugging electronics, using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rechargeable_batteries" title="Rechargeable batteries">rechargeable batteries</a>, growing plants, riding bikes, and adopting abandoned pets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-queen_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-queen-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pasta2_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-pasta2-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The book has sold over 40,000 copies and is on its fourth printing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>She recorded the duet "I Think I Love You" for <a href="/wiki/K-pop" title="K-pop">K-pop</a> singer MY Q's 2011 album <i>Ready for the World</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gong, along with fellow actresses <a href="/wiki/Kim_Min-hee_(actress,_born_1982)" title="Kim Min-hee (actress, born 1982)">Kim Min-hee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Choi_Kang-hee_(actress)" title="Choi Kang-hee (actress)">Choi Kang-hee</a>, is considered an influential fashion icon by Korean women in their 20s and 30s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In 2010 she collaborated with shoe brand pushBUTTON to produce the capsule collection "Excuse Me + pushBUTTON." Two years later, Suecomma Bonnie, another shoe brand, released her "Excuse Me x Suecomma Bonnie" line. In 2012 she designed a selected line of apparel for the fashion shop Los Angeles Project, a brand located inside <a href="/wiki/Shinsegae" title="Shinsegae">Shinsegae</a> Department Stores. Her neon-colored and patterned items were sold under the name "LAP by Kong Hyo-jin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>She was named jury president for the 2009 <a href="/wiki/SEOUL_International_Women%27s_Film_Festival" title="SEOUL International Women's Film Festival">International Women's Film Festival in Seoul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Gong was also a jury member for the 2011 Asiana International Short Film Festival, and the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Mise-en-sc%C3%A8ne_Short_Film_Festival" title="Mise-en-scène Short Film Festival">Mise-en-scène Short Film Festival</a>.</p>
